A list of novels, sorted by difficulty

The Score is the average character frequency index. To illustrate, the first 7
characters in Patrick  Zein's frequency list are
| Char | I(Char) |
| 的   |       1 |
| 一   |       2 |
| 是   |       3 |
| 不   |       4 |
| 了   |       5 |
| 人   |       6 |
| 我   |       7 |

So the Score of the sentence 我不是人 Would be
  (I(我) + I(不) + I(是) + I(人))/(Char count)
=  (7 + 4 + 3 + 6)/4
=  5

This number means that on average, each character read in this sentence will be
the 5th most frequent character. This formula has the property of associativity,
meaning, for example, that the sentence 我不是人！我不是人！, has the same score
of 5 as the sentence 我不是人.  Suppose we have two books of difficulty x. If we
cut the covers off them and glued them together, considering them as one book,
would we consider this book to be more difficult than the two original books?
The assumption behind the equation used is that this new book would simply be
"more material of the same difficulty", hence the associativity equation:

Score(A) + Score(B) = Score(AB)

This argument can then be used in reverse, considering a single book cut in
half, the score of the first and second halves ought to add up to equal the
total. This can be repeated until we have cut the book up into the smallest
measurable unit; in this case, a single chinese character.

The table below is the haodoo.net corpus of novels, sorted by this score, as of
December 2016. It can be seen that the unique character count varies greatly,
but does so in conjunction with the char count. This illustrates the bias such a
measure has towards longer novels being considered more difficult, due to
naturally containing more characters. The average character frequency index on
the other hand gives as a number more-or-less representing how many new words we
expect to encounter per sentence or page.
